General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Stephen Dean; Alice Williams; Edward A L Turner ♥ [1]

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ 42 N ♥

♠ Alternative names ♣ Hepthalite Huns; Ye-Ta; Cao; Ephthalites; Hayathelaites; Hephtal; He-ta; Hoa; Hoa-Tun; Hunas; Iranian Huns; the people of Hua; White Huns; Yeda; Ye-tai; Ye-ti-i-li-do ♥

Cao; Ephthalites; Hayathelaites; Hephtal; He-ta; Hoa; Hoa-Tun; Hunas; Iranian Huns; the people of Hua; White Huns; Yeda; Ye-tai; Ye-ti-i-li-do. [2]

♠ Peak Date ♣ 509-522 CE ♥

509 CE. The apex of Hephthalite power. Over forty countries paid tribute, and the authority of the tribal confederacy extended over a vast territorial area from central Asia into northern India. [3]

"522 The height of Hephthalite power."[4]

Reign of King Toramana. When Toramana "died sometime between 515 and 520 and was succeeded by his son, crown prince Mihirakula, the nature of the empire changed."[5]

Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 408-561 CE ♥

The beginning data, 408 CE, marks the first appearance of the polity as a separate entity in the records of the local empires when they begin raiding the Sassanian Empire.

"557-61 Final victory of Khosrow I, the Sassanid king, over Hephthalite forces."[6]

Between 557 CE to 561 CE the Persian King Chosroes allied with another steppe people who had appeared from inner Asia. Although some component peoples of the Hepthalites may have survived into the period of the Islamic conquest, even this contingent had faded outside of some mountain strongholds by around 670 CE.[7]

"565 Almost complete disappearance of Hephthalites in the face of the emergence of the western Turks (Gokturks)."[8]

570 CE is when the core territories were split between the Turkic nomads in the north, and the resurgent Sassanian empire.

The timeline of Hepathalites arrival into India is murky but indicates a period of dominance until the local population rebelled after religious persecution and a reduction in military support when the Hepthalites faced conflicts with incoming Turkic peoples. [9]


♠ Degree of centralization ♣ nominal; confederated state ♥

The Hepthalites were one group of a series of nomadic tribal confederations that are sometimes referred to as the White Huns. The evidence seems to indicate that they were a second wave of Hunnish migration. Commentators at the time differ as to what the structure of the group was and to what degree they differed from the other nomadic peoples of the area. The Byzantian commentator Procopius of Caesarea stressed that, 'They are not nomadic like the other Hunnish peoples, but have long since settled on fertile land.' He further explained that unlike the other peoples of central asia, the Hepthalites were, 'ruled by one king and possess a legal state structure, observing justice among themselves and with their neighbours in no lesser measure than the Byzantines and Persians.' [10]

As a nomadic confederation, there does not seem to have been a centralized power structure, although some evidence indicates the adoption of local administrations for the purposes of exacting tribute. [11] During the peak of their power, they seem to have become increasingly sedentary, and this potentially increased the degree of centralization.

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance; vassalage ♥

Sassanid tribute

"459 Hephthalites assist Firuz (Peroz) to regain his Sassanid throne; he must pay significant tribute in return." [12]

none: 408-562 CE; vassalage: 563-670 CE [13]

The Hepthalite were vassals to Kusrau I, the Sasasian King. Independent elements in the south maintained a degree of independence in the Zerafshan valley as tribute payers to the Turks, and further south to the Sasasians. By the 570s the only truly independent fragments of the former power of the Hepthalites were located in modern day Tajikistan and Afghanistan, the longest enduring in Kabul.[14]

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Sassanid Empire I ♥ the core area of this polity is considered to be Sogdiana
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ elite migration ♥ "The original homeland of the Hephthalites is relatively obscure although most experts agree that they originated north of the Great Wall of China, in or near present day Mongolia. A Chinese source from the second century states that they lived in a region of northwest China sometimes referred to as Dzungaria, a steppe area surrounded by mountain chains."[15] All the nomadic kingdoms that ﬂourished in Bactria between the middle of the fourth century CE and the middle of the sixth century CE seem to have originated in a massive migration in the second half of the fourth century between 350 CE and 370 CE. [16]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ First Turk Khaganate ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Steppe nomads ♥ Hephthalites "of uncertain origin and cultural affiliation"[17]
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ [7,500,000-8,000,000] ♥ km squared. "The Byzantine writer Procopius in the early sixth century refers to them as white-bodied Huns ... However, there is no material or linguistic evidence that they were related to the Huns or Xiongnu at all, and the name has generally been interpreted as a mistaken identity given to a nomadic people whose culture resembled that of the Huns."[18] All the nomadic kingdoms that ﬂourished in Bactria between the middle of the fourth century CE and the middle of the sixth century CE seem to have originated in a massive migration in the second half of the fourth century between 350 CE and 370 CE. [19]

♠ Capital ♣ Balkh; Pendjikent; Sakala ♥ attested as the capital by Chinese sources from the period, although how centralized the state was is largely unknown. Some limited excavation has taken place. [20]

"473-79 Hephthalites conquer Sogdiana and establish a capital city at Pendjikent."[21]

Toramana, who made conquests in India "established his capital city at Salaka" [22]

♠ Language ♣ Bactrian ♥ Bactrian; Iranian; Turkic According to the Chinese records from the period, the language of the Hephthalites was distinct from that of those Iranian-speaking people of Central Asia who were called Hu by the Chinese. However, there is no consensus among scholars.[23] Recent work has reappraised Chinese manuscript sources to postulate that the Hephtalites had ceased to retain their original Altaic language and adopted Bactrian by the end of the fourth century. [24] "Probably dominated by an Eastern Iranian language, but their mixed ancestry also lead to multilingualism."[25] ♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥

♠ Religion Genus ♣ Hephthalite Religions ♥ "Information about the religion of the Hephthalites is provided by the Chinese sources. Sung Yun reports that in Tokharistan ‘the majority of them do not believe in Buddhism. Most of them worship wai-shen or “foreign gods”.’ He makes almost identical remarks about the Hephthalites of Gandhara, saying that they honour kui-shen (demons). The manuscripts of the Liang shu (Book 54) contain important evidence: ‘[the Hephthalites] worship T’ien- shen or [the] heaven god and Huo-shen or [the] fire god. Every morning they first go outside [of their tents] and pray to [the] gods and then take breakfast.’ For the Chinese observer, the heaven god and the fire god were evidently foreign gods. We have no evidence of the specific content of these religious beliefs but it is quite possible that they belonged to the Iranian (or Indo-Iranian) group."[26]

Map[edit]

Hepthalite Empire 500 CE [27]


General Description[edit]

The Hepthalites were one group of a series of nomadic tribal confederations that are sometimes referred to as the White Huns. The evidence seems to indicate that they were a second wave of Hunnish migration.[28] Despite the name, some scholars think the White Huns were in fact not a Turkic people, but rather the easternmost group of Iranian nomads.[29]

At their peak territorial coverage the Hephthalite lands may have enclosed 26 million people but most subjects likely had a great deal of autonomy. As a nomadic confederation, the Hepthalites may not have possessed a central administration, although evidence indicates at least the adoption of local administrations for the purposes of exacting tribute.[30] During the peak of their power, they seem to have become increasingly sedentary, and this may have increased the degree of centralization. Coins show Hephthalites practiced skull deformation[31] which may provide indirect evidence for strong social hierarchy and status competition.

Commentators at the time differed in their opinions as to what the structure of the group was and to what degree they differed from the other nomadic peoples of the area. The Byzantian commentator Procopius of Caesarea stressed that, 'They are not nomadic like the other Hunnish peoples, but have long since settled on fertile land.' He further explained that unlike the other peoples of central asia, the Hepthalites were,'ruled by one king and possess a legal state structure, observing justice among themselves and with their neighbours in no lesser measure than the Byzantines and Persians.'[32]

Social Complexity variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Stephen Dean; Alice Williams ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 1,000,000: 450 CE; [2,750,000-3,000,000]: 500 CE ♥ squared kilometers,


In 532 CE India was lost after a successful rebellion. In 557 CE - 561 CE the Sasasanids annexed substantial territories, and in 565 CE the Goturks claimed the remainder and took a number of successor kingdoms as vassals.

The late ﬁfth and early sixth centuries saw the start of Hephthalite raids on Gandhara and northern India.

Information is scarce, but recent discoveries have given evidence of what had been earlier only speculation. Islamic sources describe a series of disastrous campaigns by the Sassasian King Peroz that resulted in his eventual death.[34] Following this victory, the Hepthalties secured control over Central Asia, Korasan and Afganistan from the Sassanian and Kushan Kingdoms.[35] They continued to expand until the sixth century, when they were defeated by a recovered Sassasian Empire fighting alongside another confederation of central Asian nomads known as the Gokturks. This defeat destroyed Hepthalite power in transoxania, though some fragment of power seems to have existed until the Arab invasions.[36]

♠ Polity Population ♣ 26,500,000: 500 CE ♥

The total size of the Hephthalite population is unknown. In the territory of Tokharistan there were reportedly 5,000-6,000 Hephthalite warriors. This could suggest a population of 50,000 individuals if the extended family groups were included, although this would have been experienced considerable ﬂuctuations with the increase and decrease of the number of affiliated tribes and fortune of the Hepthalite. [37]

McEvedy and Jones (1978) 26,500,000: 500 CE [38]

Russian Tukestan 1,000,000: 400-600 CE
Pakistan, India and Bangladesh: 45,000,000: 400 CE; 50,000,000: 500 CE. In 200 BC approximately 40% in the Ganges Basin which would translate to 20m in 500 CE. Hephthalites held Upper and Middle Gangers Basin so perhaps two-thirds (13m) under Hephthalite control. Indus Basin likely to have been next most populous part of Indian sub-continent at this time. If 25% of the total: 12.5m.

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ ♥

Likely to be Samarkand? < 50,000

"The Suishu tells us that the capital city of the Hephthalites measured about 10 Chinese miles square."[39]

Toramana, who made conquests in India "established his capital city at Salaka, and in a brief time Salaka grew into a metropolis, 'a great centre of trade'." [40]

The Questions of King Milinda on Salaka: "in parks and gardens and groves and lakes and tanks, a paradise of rivers and mountains and woods. Wise architects have laid it out ... strong towers and ramparts, with superb gates and entrance archways; and with the royal citadel in its midst, white walled and deeply moated. Well laid out are its streets, squares, cross roads, and market places. ... And there is laid up there much store of property and corn and things of value in warehouses - foods and drinks of every sort, syrups and sweetmeats of every kind."[41]

"one of the largest towns was Balkh... had about 100 Buddhist vihāras (monasteries) and some 3000 monks." [42]

Balkh, Termez, and Budrach were other cities in the territory, but the actual number of inhabitants in unknown. A Chinese observer remarked that the cities were heavily fortified but not densely populated. [43]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ 2000 ♥ 2000km from a capital in Sogdiana to Ganges Basin.

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [2-3] ♥ [44] The levels potentially consisted of:

1. Fortified Urban communities: Balkh, Termez. Balkh described as having 3,000 monks. It had a circumference of c. 20 li. Temez had "perhaps 1, 000 monks." It had a circumference of 20 li. The area of the 'town' is 10 ha. The area of the town plus suburb is 70 ha.[45]

2. Subjugated agricultural villages
3. Nomadic peoples 'Without cities and towns, they follow water and grass, using felt to make tents, moving to the cold places in summer, to the warm ones in winter.' [46]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [4-5] ♥

Hephthalites produced coins. On that basis:

1. King

2. Advisor or government official
3. Manager of a mint
4. Mint worker

In general, Hephthalite ruler maintained control over his regions through lesser kings and pre-established dynasties.


India: pre-existing infrastructure of provincial government

After the Hephthalites conquered Gupta dynasty provinces in India their rulers came under Hephthalite control.[47] So they would have inherited whatever administrative structure was present in the former Gupta provinces.

Tarim Basin: vassals not provinces

"the Hephthalites interferred minimally in the affairs of the Tarim cities after subduing them, contenting themselves with the extraction of tributes."[48]


unknown: 408-550 CE; 2: 550 CE [49]

There seems to have was a great deal of autonomy in the Hepthalite kingdoms. This was the case in Chaganiyan, on the upper and middle reaches of the Surkhan Darya. There is some speculation that local administrative structures were maintained, but this does not have enough evidence for anything beyond the extraction of tribute. There is also some limited evidence of the titles of officials from gemstones. [50]

Small family landholdings

Fraternal polyandry marriage "it was the custom for women to adorn their hats with horns, one per husband." "If a man had no brothers he would often adopt another man so as to be able to marry." More recently Tibetans who practiced this form of marriage did it "to make sure that small family landholdings did not have to be divided among brothers. Instead all male offspring remained on their parents' land and worked it together as a single landholding. This system also limits population growth significantly since each generation produces the children of only one woman instead of offspring from the wives of all brothers." However, since the Hephthalite nomads did not have family farms they may have practiced this form of marriage for keeping the herds together and population control. Chinese records don't mention status of the presumably many unmarried Hephthalite women.[51]


♠ Religious levels ♣ 1 ♥ The city of Balkh had about 100 Buddhist monasteries and some 3,000 monks. Outside the town was a large Buddhist monastery, although this description occurs after the area had been conquered by the Turks. Buddhism, Zoroastrianism, Christianity, a Sun cult and some kind of sky and fire worship are also attested. However, we have no information as to what the Hepthalites practiced, and whether this had a hierarchical structure. [52]

"There is some evidence that Buddhism was practices in some of the territories held by the Hephthalites; however, some contemporary authors also wrote of the persecution of Buddhists. There are references to sacred fire, which indicates at least some familiarity with Zoroastrianism. However, because Hephthalite graves have been found, not all aspects of Zoroastrianism would have been practiced, since the funerary ritual of this religion, entailed leaving the body in the open to be devoured by sacred birds and dogs. As was the case with language religion was probably another area in which the Hephthalites constituted a multicultural society".[53]

♠ Military levels ♣ 4 ♥ [54] This is a tentative estimate as the sources are not clear as to what the actual structure of the Hepthalite military was, although some terms for ranks are preserved. The ranks below are based on Bactrian seals found at several archaeological sites.

1. King

2. Asbarobido 'Chief of cavalry'
3. Oazarko fromalaro 'Great Commander'
4. Military serving tribesman

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥ [55] Seals in Bactrian seem to indicate that there were professional officers and officials.

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ [56]

As a nomadic people, every male member of the tribe was expected to be a professional fighter. The Hepthalites are described as having inspired dread in those tasked with facing them in battle, a testimony to their effectiveness.

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ Theodore: "Appointed Nestorian Christian archbishop at Merv in 540." [57]

A number of competing faiths were present, but there is no clear evidence of what faith was sanctioned or supported by the ruling elites. One Chinese commentator from the period remarked that the Hephthalites of Gandhara 'honoured kui-shên (demons).' [58]

"There is some evidence that Buddhism was practices in some of the territories held by the Hephthalites; however, some contemporary authors also wrote of the persecution of Buddhists. There are references to sacred fire, which indicates at least some familiarity with Zoroastrianism. However, because Hephthalite graves have been found, not all aspects of Zoroastrianism would have been practiced, since the funerary ritual of this religion, entailed leaving the body in the open to be devoured by sacred birds and dogs. As was the case with language religion was probably another area in which the Hephthalites constituted a multicultural society".[59]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ {absent; present} ♥ unknown [60] imitations of Sassanian coinage were made. [61] - surely a mint is evidence for a full-time government employee, at least for the individual who ran the mint.

♠ Source of support ♣ suspected unknown ♥ [62]

♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ Inferred as the state institutions are described as displaying their origins in "ancestral tribal arrangements" i.e. without characteristics like exams. [63]

♠ Merit promotion ♣ inferred absent ♥ As a tribal confederacy, promotion was based on individual ability within the tribal structure. However, the stratification of the ruling elites seems to have taken place with increasing sedentary lifestyles, especially in India. A Chinese account states that the throne of the Hephthalites, ‘was not transmitted by inheritance but awarded to the most capable kinsman’ [64] Does not qualify as regular, institutionalized procedures for promotion based on performance.

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ The historical consensus is divided as to whether they were even sedentary, let alone using specialized buildings.[65] The Hephthalites made use of specialized buildings for governmental purposes. They had coins so at the least they had or commissioned the use of mints for coinage.

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ ♥ unknown [66]

Law. There is no evidence as to what, if any legal code was followed. Although Byzantine chroniclers indicated that the Hepthalites, 'had a system based on law', but Chinese sources contradict this and state that the Hepthalites maintained good order by a constantly patrolling army. [67]

♠ Judges ♣ ♥ unknown [68]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ present ♥ Chinese chroniclers indicate that the Hepthalites were a non-literate people, although they were described as having used tally sticks.
♠ Non written records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ "Examples of the Hephthalite written language have been discovered in East Turkestan, Central Asia, Afghanistan and northern Pakistan. However, these are only insignificant vestiges of the large quantity of written material which, if we are to believe Hsüan-tsang, was to be found in the regions occupied by the Hephthalites and particularly in Tokharistan" [79]
♠ Script ♣ absent: 408-487 CE; present: 488-670 CE ♥ Local scripts were in use in subjugated territories, but there is a controversy over whether the Hepthalites were a literate people. Bactrian, Pahlavi, Kharos and Brahmi were in use by others. [80]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ absent ♥
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ inferred present ♥ Indo-European.

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ absent: 408-487 CE; present: 488-561 CE ♥ [81] As little is known about the Hepthalitess written records, the coding below reflects areas they conquered from the Sasasian empire and the territories taken in India.
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ absent: 408-487 CE; present: 488-670 CE [82] Khwarazm region: "The Khwarazmian solar calendar, related to the Zoroastrian system, is known to us thanks to Biruni, who argued that it was in advance of most other ancient systems for measuring time." [83]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ absent: 408-487 CE; present: 488-561 CE ♥ [84] Khwarazm region: "The Khwarazmian solar calendar, related to the Zoroastrian system, is known to us thanks to Biruni, who argued that it was in advance of most other ancient systems for measuring time." [85]
♠ Religious literature ♣ absent: 408-487 CE; present: 488-670 CE ♥ [86] Topics of texts included: commentaries on Avesta; philosophy and debate; apocalyptic; didactic; geographical and epic; legal; cultural and dictionaries. [87] The Original Creation "subject-matter ranges from cosmology, astronomy and eschatology to lists of rivers, mountains and plants." [88] Religious Judgments by Manuchihr. Answered 92 questions on Zoroastrian belief. [89] "Sometime around 518, a Buddhist mission came to the north of India from China, searching for scriptures to collect and preserve. According to their own records, they managed to leave India with 170 volumes."[90] Bozorghmer (531-578 CE): "Native of Merv and the best-known Central Asian thinker of the pre-Islamic era. A Zoroastrian dualist, Bozorghmer propounded ideas on ethics that influenced thinkers deep into the Muslim age. He also served as vizier and invented the game of backgammon."[91]
♠ Practical literature ♣ present ♥ Manuals for games, like chess (translated from India).[92]
♠ History ♣ present ♥ Khwaday-namag, Yazdgird III. History of creation to Khosrau II from a Zoroastrian perspective.[93]
♠ Philosophy ♣ present ♥ Works translated from Romans. [94] Bozorghmer (531-578 CE): "Native of Merv and the best-known Central Asian thinker of the pre-Islamic era. A Zoroastrian dualist, Bozorghmer propounded ideas on ethics that influenced thinkers deep into the Muslim age. He also served as vizier and invented the game of backgammon."[95]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ present ♥ Astronomy, medicine, mirrors (translated from India). [96] Medical treatises (translated from Romans).[97]
♠ Fiction ♣ present ♥ Fables and stories translated from India included the Book of Sindbad, Seventy Tales of the Parrot and Kalilag u Dimnag and Bilauhar u Budasaf (which concerned the Buddha). Vis u Ramin (a Parthian origin tale) and Vamiq u Adhra (a Greek story) were translated into Pahlavi in this period. [98]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ inferred present ♥ required for bronze
♠ Bronze ♣ present♥ Bronze had been used on the central steppes from 1500 BCE. [103]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ Iron had been used in by the steppe nomads from 330 BCE - 200 BCE. [104]
♠ Steel ♣ present ♥ At this time in Central Asia if high-quality steel was used it would have been imported. The following sources suggest later dates for fine steel. However we code present because the Hephthalites occupied northern India (a location repeatedly associated with fine steel) which as early as 1st CE was exporting iron and steel as far as East Africa.[105] “In the context of this work, it is important to note that crucible steel of fine quality was made at Herat, in Bukhara and in northern India.”[106] Reference for high quality of the steel (this one dates from 900 CE): "Further east from Merv along the Silk Road is a region praised for its iron and steel production by Greek, Islamic, and Chinese writers. The Sogdian state of Ustrushana, a mountainous region east of Samarkand, and the Ferghana basin ... material related to the medieval iron and steel industry has been uncovered here. Most relevant ... is a workshop excavated at a city-site of the +9th-13th centuries in Feghana, at Eski Achsy, Uzbekistan. ..” Crucible fragments ”The excavators consider that the process used here was direct production of steel from ore, just as He Tangkun argues for the Luoyang crucibles. It is quite possible, however, that they were (also) used in co-fusion steel production as suggested by the Merv excavators."[107] Fine steel swords may have been produced at an earlier time than 900 CE with the technology coming from northern India or from this region via Persia: In Tibet c700 CE "steel swords were certainly available through trade with Sogdia and Fergana ... and many steel blades are known from Central Asia from the late first millennium until the arrival of Genghis Khan in the early thirteenth century."[108] "The Sogdian cities of Samarqand and Bukhara probably also manufactured iron and steel weapons that were exported to Tibet. We know that by the early eighth century, the Sogdians, having probably borrowed the technology from the Sasanians, were manufacturing mail armor and offered suits of the material as gifts to the Tang court in 718. ... The Sasasnians may themselves have developed knowledge of steelmaking from contacts with northern India."[109] "The principal centres for the manufacture of steel weapons in Central Asia were Khwarazm, Ferghana and northern India.”[110]

Projectiles[edit]

♠ Javelins ♣ present ♥ "There are a number of artistic depictions, from different eras, that show steppe warriors on horseback and armed with a javelin". [111] Bone-tipped javelins are less likely to leave finds for archaeologists. "Like the Mongols they were a race of horsemen. They fought with bone-tipped javelins, with sabers, and with slings or lassoes. They ate herbs and half- raw meat, which they first used as saddles ; and they clothed themselves with the skins of wild animals”.
♠ Other thrown weapons ♣ present ♥ the lasso [112]
♠ Atlatl ♣ absent ♥ Weapon of the Americas, extremely unlikely to have been present here
♠ Slings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Powerful composite bows suggest these weapons had become obsolete
♠ Self bow ♣ inferred absent ♥ Powerful composite bows suggest these weapons had become obsolete
♠ Composite bow ♣ present ♥ The central Asian compound bow was made using sinew, wood and horn to produce a weapon with deadly penetrative power and range. [113]
♠ Crossbow ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in previous and subsequent polities.
♠ Tension siege engines ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in previous and subsequent polities.
♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥ First use of the counter-weight trebuchet 1165 CE at Byzantine siege of Zevgminon.[114]
♠ Incendiaries ♣ absent ♥ 'Persians threw vessels filled with sulphur, asphalt and naphtha into the enemy bulwarks. The first mention of the common place use of naphtha as an incendiary device in the annals of Islam is in connection with a raid in India in 777. During the siege of Heraklia in 803, however, stone balls wrapped in linen and covered with burning naphtha were hurled by mangonels against the ramparts. The fire would cling to the stones and cause them to burst. From that time onwards, the caliphate employed corps of naphtha-throwers and the Muslim fleets were provided with it as well. In order to protect themselves, people covered flammable materials with newly flayed animal skins and those who handled the naphtha wore clothes saturated with talc. Naphtha-filled glass grenades appeared first in 934, unless in that year the weapons were actually incendiary javelins, but the use of fire grenades was firmly established a few years later. ... The latest historian of Greek fire, Maurice Mercier, admits that during the thirteenth century, people began to use grenades filled with mealed gunpowder, called 'Syrian jugs', which had a most explosive force. [115]
♠ Gunpowder siege artillery ♣ absent ♥ Inferred as came later in history. [116]
♠ Handheld firearms ♣ absent ♥ Inferred as came later in history. [117]

Handheld weapons[edit]

♠ War clubs ♣ inferred present ♥ The Hepthalites were steppe nomads who adopted the patterns of warfare suited to the central Asian steppe. Although direct evidence is scant, descriptions seem to indicate that they were mounted archers who may had utilized the stirrup and the double sheath. Evidence of there equipment is hard to verify, although they seem to have relied on mounted forces and traditional steppe tactics. There is some evidence of club use by the infantry. [118]
♠ Battle axes ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in preceding and succeeding polities.
♠ Daggers ♣ present ♥ Murals at Dilberjin near Balkh (5th-7th century) shows men armed with daggers. [119] "Among the steppe riders a dagger was typically carried in all periods, and a number of dagger designs are encountered in the archaeological and artistic record." [120]
♠ Swords ♣ present ♥ According to Litvinsky,[121] their main weapon was the sword.
♠ Spears ♣ present ♥ At Gurgan (484 CE) "the Hephthalite king stuck the treaty that Peroz had signed, promising not to invade Hephthalite territory again, on the tip of his spear."[122]
♠ Polearms ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in preceding and succeeding polities.

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Gupta Empire. Sogdiana. Khorasan. Sasanids. Göktürks.

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ Buddhists.

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Military revolts ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ present ♥ Governor of Malwa rebelled against Mihirakula in 528 CE and "was able to drive the enemy back up into the northern reaches of the Punjab."[152]

♠ rural succession ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Violent riots urban ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Oppression / extermination of subaltern group ♣ present ♥ King Mihirakula "decided to enforce a new kind of orthodoxy; to get rid of Buddhism. [....] [H]e saw Buddhism as the enemy of his power, most likely because it was the religion of the Guptas [...] destroy[ing] Buddhist temples, monasteries and books.[153]

Cultural distance[edit]

♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ E.g. against Gupta polity (Sanskrit, Prakrit[154]). Recent work has reappraised Chinese manuscript sources to postulate that the Hephtalites had ceased to retain their original Altaic language and adopted Bactrian by the end of the fourth century. [155] "Probably dominated by an Eastern Iranian language, but their mixed ancestry also lead to multilingualism."[156]

♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ present ♥ E.g. against the Turks (Turkic). Recent work has reappraised Chinese manuscript sources to postulate that the Hephtalites had ceased to retain their original Altaic language and adopted Bactrian by the end of the fourth century. [157] "Probably dominated by an Eastern Iranian language, but their mixed ancestry also lead to multilingualism."[158] Bactrian is an Indo-European language.

♠ Wars between groups from different archaeological cultures ♣ present ♥ E.g. South Asian polities.

♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Information about the religion of the Hephthalites is provided by the Chinese sources. Sung Yun reports that in Tokharistan ‘the majority of them do not believe in Buddhism. Most of them worship wai-shen or “foreign gods”.’ He makes almost identical remarks about the Hephthalites of Gandhara, saying that they honour kui-shen (demons). The manuscripts of the Liang shu (Book 54) contain important evidence: ‘[the Hephthalites] worship T’ien- shen or [the] heaven god and Huo-shen or [the] fire god. Every morning they first go outside [of their tents] and pray to [the] gods and then take breakfast.’ For the Chinese observer, the heaven god and the fire god were evidently foreign gods. We have no evidence of the specific content of these religious beliefs but it is quite possible that they belonged to the Iranian (or Indo-Iranian) group."[159]

♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ absent ♥ "Information about the religion of the Hephthalites is provided by the Chinese sources. Sung Yun reports that in Tokharistan ‘the majority of them do not believe in Buddhism. Most of them worship wai-shen or “foreign gods”.’ He makes almost identical remarks about the Hephthalites of Gandhara, saying that they honour kui-shen (demons). The manuscripts of the Liang shu (Book 54) contain important evidence: ‘[the Hephthalites] worship T’ien- shen or [the] heaven god and Huo-shen or [the] fire god. Every morning they first go outside [of their tents] and pray to [the] gods and then take breakfast.’ For the Chinese observer, the heaven god and the fire god were evidently foreign gods. We have no evidence of the specific content of these religious beliefs but it is quite possible that they belonged to the Iranian (or Indo-Iranian) group."[160] Previously coded "present", but for this variable to be present the Hepthalites would have followed a world religion and fought a polity that followed a different world religion.

♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ present ♥ E.g. against South Asian polities (Hinduism, Buddhism). "Information about the religion of the Hephthalites is provided by the Chinese sources. Sung Yun reports that in Tokharistan ‘the majority of them do not believe in Buddhism. Most of them worship wai-shen or “foreign gods”.’ He makes almost identical remarks about the Hephthalites of Gandhara, saying that they honour kui-shen (demons). The manuscripts of the Liang shu (Book 54) contain important evidence: ‘[the Hephthalites] worship T’ien- shen or [the] heaven god and Huo-shen or [the] fire god. Every morning they first go outside [of their tents] and pray to [the] gods and then take breakfast.’ For the Chinese observer, the heaven god and the fire god were evidently foreign gods. We have no evidence of the specific content of these religious beliefs but it is quite possible that they belonged to the Iranian (or Indo-Iranian) group."[161]

♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Previously coded "present" with "Scythians" as the sole example, but no evidence could be found in the literature consulted for a war or wars between Hephthalites and Scythians.

♠ Wars on steppe frontier ♣ present ♥ E.g. against the Turks in the 560s CE. "War became inevitable and the Turks invaded Hephthalite territory."[162]

♠ Wars on agricultural frontier ♣ inferred absent ♥ No hunter-gatherers in this region.

Intensity of warfare[edit]

♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥ E.g. the late fifth century CE "was the low point of Sasanian rule, where they in fact became tributaries to the Hephthalites and ceded territory to them for returning the king and his entourage to Sasanian territory."[163]

♠ Looting ♣ present ♥ "Throughout most of the 420s nomadic Hephthalite raiders invaded Sassanid territories many times, plundering crops and herds as well as wreaking havoc."[164]

♠ Sack ♣ inferred present ♥ "Throughout most of the 420s nomadic Hephthalite raiders invaded Sassanid territories many times, plundering crops and herds as well as wreaking havoc."[165]

♠ Rape ♣ inferred present ♥ "Throughout most of the 420s nomadic Hephthalite raiders invaded Sassanid territories many times, plundering crops and herds as well as wreaking havoc."[166]

♠ Deportation ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Expulsion ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Enslavement ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarites captured by the Sassanids were likely enslaved. "Slavery was more widespread in the Sasanid period. There were different categories of slave: bandag (servant); ansahrig (outlander); wardag (captive). The servant was usually in the household or a fire temple. The ansahrig usually worked on a farm and was non-Zoroastrian. A wardag worked on the land, in construction or other manual labor."[167]

♠ Imprisonment ♣ present ♥ Sassanid king Peroz "was captured and had to pay a large ransom." [168] Persian prisoners taken in 483 CE.[169]

♠ Destruction ♣ present ♥ "Throughout most of the 420s nomadic Hephthalite raiders invaded Sassanid territories many times, plundering crops and herds as well as wreaking havoc."[170] In northern India cities destroyed in Hephthalite wars.[171] King Mihirakula "decided to enforce a new kind of orthodoxy; to get rid of Buddhism. ... he saw Buddhism as the enemy of his power, most likely because it was the religion of the Guptas." He destroyed temples, monasteries and books.[172]

♠ Torture ♣ suspected unknown ♥ King Mihirakula "decided to enforce a new kind of orthodoxy; to get rid of Buddhism. ... he saw Buddhism as the enemy of his power, most likely because it was the religion of the Guptas." Buddhist missionaries said of King Mihirakula: "The disposition of this king was cruel and vindictive", "he practised the most barbarous atrocities."[173] It is possible these "barbarous atrocities" included torture, but far from certain.

♠ Mutilation ♣ suspected unknown ♥ King Mihirakula "decided to enforce a new kind of orthodoxy; to get rid of Buddhism. ... he saw Buddhism as the enemy of his power, most likely because it was the religion of the Guptas." Buddhist missionaries said of King Mihirakula: "The disposition of this king was cruel and vindictive", "he practised the most barbarous atrocities."[174] It is possible these "barbarous atrocities" included mutilation, but far from certain.

♠ Targeted massacre ♣ inferred present ♥ Persecution of Buddhists. Buddhist missionaries on King Mihirakula: "The disposition of this king was cruel and vindictive", "he practised the most barbarous atrocities."[175]

♠ General massacre ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Persecution of Buddhists. Buddhist missionaries on King Mihirakula: "The disposition of this king was cruel and vindictive", "he practised the most barbarous atrocities."[176]

♠ Extermination ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Ethnocide ♣ inferred present ♥ King Mihirakula "decided to enforce a new kind of orthodoxy; to get rid of Buddhism. ... he saw Buddhism as the enemy of his power, most likely because it was the religion of the Guptas." He destroyed temples, monasteries and books.[177]

List of Wars[edit]

"420-27 Hephthalites raid Persian Sassanid empire until they suffer defeat in 427." [178]

"454 Hephthalites avenge their previous defeat at the hands of the Sassanids." [179]

"465-70 Hephthalites conquer Indian Gandhara."[180]

"470-80 Indo-Hephthalites wage war against the Indian Gupta dynasty; upon their victory Hephthalites claim territory in all of northern and central India."[181]

"473-79 Hephthalites conquer Sogdiana and establish a capital city at Pendjikent."[182]

"484-86 Hephthalites are militarily successful against the Persian Sassanids and turn them into a vassal state, temporarily."[183]

"532 Hephthalites are driven out of India by a coalition of Indian forces."[184]

"557-61 Final victory of Khosrow I, the Sassanid king, over Hephthalite forces."[185]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ No Roz ♥ No Roz was "the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force", and it involved several days of feasting, gift-exchange, and general merrymaking [187]. Many different religions were practiced within the confines of the Hephthalite Empire, but it appears that Zoroastrianism and Buddhism were the most widespread. Because what little is known about the religion of the dominant group, the Hephthalites themselves, suggests a closer affinity to Zoroastrianism than to Buddhism, and because contemporary sources report that, at least for a time, the Hephthalites were intolerant towards Buddhism in Northern India [188], it seems most appropriate here to code the largest Zoroastrian ritual, rather than the largest Buddhist one. it would have been better, of course, to code a ritual that was unique to the Hephthalites themselves, but very little is known about Hephthalite ritual in general, and the largest known Hephthalite ritual, depicted in a mural in the fifth- or sixt-century fortress of Balalyk-Tepe, only shows fifty or so participants [189]. Regrettably, it is also not possible to know how exactly No Roz was performed at this time, and whether it differed in interesting ways from the idealised account [190] that will be used here as a basis for coding.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [191].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [192].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [193]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual is described as having 'mandatory participation', it seems there is no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'
♠ Duration ♣ 24 ♥ hours on basis ritual is for the whole day one day. [194]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ No Roz was one of the seven Zoroastrian festivals to demand mandatory participation [195].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was one of the seven Zoroastrian festivals to demand mandatory participation [196]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual is described as having 'mandatory participation', it seems there is no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'whole polity'

'Among other things which it was thought propitious to begin the day with, was a mouthful of pure fresh milk and fresh cheese; all the kings of Persia took it as a blessing.' The kings also ate on No Roz morning 'white sugar with fresh Indian nuts pared.'" [218].

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Yasna ♥ Very briefly, the Yasna is the central Zoroastrian ritual, and it consisted of the sacrifice of an animal in a specially designated place, as well as the recitation of the sacred text from which the Yasna takes its name [234]. Many different religions were practiced within the confines of the Hephthalite Empire, but it appears that Zoroastrianism and Buddhism were the most widespread. Because what little is known about the religion of the dominant group, the Hephthalites themselves, suggests a closer affinity to Zoroastrianism than to Buddhism, and because contemporary sources report that, at least for a time, the Hephthalites were intolerant towards Buddhism in Northern India [235], it seems most appropriate here to code the most widespread Zoroastrian ritual, rather than the most widespread Buddhist one. it would have been better, of course, to code a ritual that was unique to the Hephthalites themselves, but, in such a vast empire, it would not have been very widespread, and, in any case, very little is known about Hephthalite ritual in general. Regrettably, it is also not possible to know how exactly the Yasna was performed at this time, and whether it differed in interesting ways from the idealised account [236] that will be used here as a basis for coding.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ [237]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ [238]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ [239]
♠ Duration ♣ [2-3] ♥ hours. two and a half hours The liturgical text from which the Yasna takes its name is recited in full during the ritual, which means that, in modern times, the ritual lasts about two and a half hours [240]. This was probably about the same in ancient times.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [241]. No information is available regarding the number of laypeople present at the ritual. "In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[242] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[243]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priests. It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [244]. Not specified by sources--expert input needed."In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[245] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[246]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ elites ♥ clergy only. Lay people are not allowed to participate in the ritual in modern days [247], which suggests they probably weren't allowed to do so in Sasanian times either, although it seems that, at the very least, they were allowed to spectate, and partake of the sacrificial animal's meat [248].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ Lay people are not allowed to participate in the ritual in modern days [249], which suggests they probably weren't allowed to do so in Sasanian times either, although it seems that, at the very least, they were allowed to spectate, and partake of the sacrificial animal's meat [250]. Not specified by sources--expert input needed.

Y. 17.6, 14, 19; 11.10)" [273].

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ morning prayer ♥ According to an early sixth-century Chinese chronicle, the Hephthalites "worship T'ien-shen or heaven-god and Huo-shen or fire-god. Every morning they first go outside (of their tents) and pray to gods and then take breakfast. They kneel down to bow only once" [309].

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ banquet ♥ The Hephthalite fortress of Bakalyk-Tepe, built between the fifth and the sixth centuries CE, contains a room whose walls are ornamented with a mural depicting a rich banquet. Little is known about ritual practices unique to the Hephthalite Empire, and this mural is the only remaining document of a euphoric ritual. It depicts 47 people, both men and women, including servants. Many of the guests hold cups and goblets likely containing "a beverage made from grapes, pressed under the floor and kept in large vessels", that is, perhaps, wine, or a wine-like substance (it is curious that the source would use so many words instead of simply saying "wine"). The servants hold fans. All guests look ethnically alike, except for a woman who may be a visitor from China. The presence of bells and ornaments in the shape of fierce mythological creatures suggest an attempt at warding off evil spirits. The room in which the mural was found also offers details on this ritual: it contains remains of an altar, as well as "[d]ifferent vessels, cups, plates, lamps, parts of wooden musical instruments,buttons and medallions" and the jaw bones of dogs, "seeds of plants, kernels of peaches, pistachioes, almonds, grapes,melons and raisins" and the remains of "silk and wool of two kinds colored with crimson and yellow colors" All this confirms the lavishness of these banquets, and the altar and jaw bones suggest its religious significance: offerings may have been made to deities. Some sources believe the ritual was part of a fertility cult. [349]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information [350].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information [351].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ Unknown. Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information [352].
♠ Duration ♣ ♥ Unknown. Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information [353].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [40-50] ♥ 47 in absolute numbers. Number of people (including servants) depicted on the mural depicting this ritual [354].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information, although the mural does suggest that important foreigners, at least, were allowed to attend [355].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information, although the mural does suggest that important foreigners, at least, were allowed to attend [356].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Neither the archaeological nor the iconographic evidence provides this information, although the mural does suggest that important foreigners, at least, were allowed to attend [357]. Thomas Cressy: As this ritual is a banquet, based on a picture with everyone participating in some way, it is unlikely that there was a passive audience, so have coded as not applicable.

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ mourning ritual ♥ Very little is known about the ritual practices most common in the Hephthalite Empire, and there is almost no information on dysphoric rituals. It is possible that Ajivika Hinduism was practiced in the parts of Northern India where the Hepthalites held their sway, but, given the Empire's significant geographic range and diversity of religious practices, painful Ajivika initiation ordeals, though no doubt dysphoric, would not be very representative of ritual in the Empire as a whole. The closest thing to a dysphoric ritual that is unique to the Hephthalite Empire is one succinctly described by an early sixth-century Chinese source, the Liangshu: "When a parent dies, the child will cut off one of his ears." The source also implies that this act occurs during the deceased's burial, and that it is not followed by any other ritual or prolonged period of mourning: "As soon as the burial is over, things go on as normal". [389]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The original source is poor in detail [390] and mentions no ritual specialist.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Assuming one outlives both one's parents, one should eventually cut both one's ears off; however, this seems unlikely.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The original source is poor in detail [391] and mentions no audience.
♠ Duration ♣ 1 ♥ hour. The original source is poor in detail [392] and makes no reference to the ritual duration, although it implies that the ritual is quite simple and mostly involves the interment of the dead of the self-mutilation of the latter's children, which overall probably did not take more than an hour in total.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The original source is poor in detail [393].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The original source is poor in detail [394].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ children of the deceased. Ancient sources mention the existence of different funerary rituals for the wealthy and for the poor, but the ritual in question appears not to be affiliated with either group [395].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The original source is poor in detail [396].

♠ Number of power transitions ♣ suspected unknown ♥ [8 names found in literature so far] Khingila. Akhshunwar. Toramana. Mihirakula. Pravarasena (530- ? CE). Gokarna. Narendraditya. Yudhishthira. "Khingila was the first known king of the Alkhon branch of the Huns, who reigned between AD 430/44 and 490 ... According to Janos Harmatta, on the other hand, Khingila was a Hephthalite king, who acceded to the throne about AD 460/466 ... another Hephthalite king named Khihkhila Narendraditya is said to have reigned before AD 570 ... one of the successors of Mihirakula, Narendraditya son of Gokarna ..."[428] "Peroz attempted to take back those lands, but he was resoundingly defeated by a Hephthalite king called Akhshunwar (or Khushnavaz)."[429] Note: Peroz ruled 459-484 CE.[430] "In the early sixth century AD a Hephthalite sub-king by the name of Toramana ..."[431] "Under their own king, Toramana, they seized the Punjab, Kashmir, and Rajputana, a policy continued vigorously under their next king, Mihirakula, who established his capital at Sakala (modern Siakot in the Punjab, Pakistan)."[432] "Pravarasena reigned from about 530 C.E. His capital, near Srinagar in Kashmir, was named Pravarasenapura after him. He issued coins inscribed with his name. We also know the names of his successors, who formed a Hun dynasty ruling over much of northwestern India and Afghanistan until the mid-seventh century C.E."[433] "The last Hephthalite king, Yudhishthira, ruled until about 670, when he was replaced by the Turk Shahi dynasty."[434] "...the last Hephthalite king surrendered to King Khusrau of Persia sometime between 560 and 563 AD."[435]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Normative Ideology

"Information about the religion of the Hephthalites is provided by the Chinese sources. Sung Yun reports that in Tokharistan ‘the majority of them do not believe in Buddhism. Most of them worship wai-shen or “foreign gods”.’ He makes almost identical remarks about the Hephthalites of Gandhara, saying that they honour kui-shen (demons). The manuscripts of the Liang shu (Book 54) contain important evidence: ‘[the Hephthalites] worship T’ien- shen or [the] heaven god and Huo-shen or [the] fire god. Every morning they first go outside [of their tents] and pray to [the] gods and then take breakfast.’ For the Chinese observer, the heaven god and the fire god were evidently foreign gods. We have no evidence of the specific content of these religious beliefs but it is quite possible that they belonged to the Iranian (or Indo-Iranian) group.

"Although Sung Yun states that the Hephthalites did not believe in Buddhism, Buddhist religious establishments flourished in Tokharistan and other areas. In India, however, the Hephthalites showed intolerance towards Buddhist religious establishments. It may be supposed that the beliefs of the local subject populations - including Buddhism - gradually began to gain ground among the Hephthalites. Various forms of Zoroastrian beliefs were widespread in Central Asia and northern and western Afghanistan in competition with Buddhism. There were also many adherents of Hindu beliefs in Afghanistan and in Tokharistan. Lastly, Manichaeism had taken firm root and Christianity was spreading.

"Chinese sources provide the following account of the funeral rites mentioned above: ‘if a man dies, a wealthy family will pile up stones to form a house [to keep the corpse]; a poor family will dig the ground for burial. The articles of everyday use are buried with the dead.’ Another source describes a third type of burial: ‘In burying the dead, the coffin is laid in a wooden case. When a parent dies, the child will cut off one of his ears.’ It is known, however, that various types of burial structure, including small, surface-level stone houses, pit graves and wooden coffins, were employed at the same period in Ferghana; hence the hypothesis that these Chinese accounts are actually descriptions of life in Ferghana.

"Polyandry was the Hephthalites’ most noteworthy social custom. Brothers had one wife in common and the children were considered as belonging to the oldest brother. The number of ‘horns’ on a married woman’s headdress corresponded to the number of her husbands. This custom was practised in ancient times among the Central Asian Saka people, the Massagetae (Herodotus, I, 216); in medieval Afghanistan (according to al-Biruni); and among present-day Tibetans." [436]

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ When sacking neighboring groups, the Hephthalite rulers took symbols of religious power to legitimate their own power. "Then *Hashnawâz, king of the Hephthalites came, killed Pêrôz, and carried off Kawâd and his sister and a fire ashostages to the Hephthalites."[437]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "As is the case with many aspects of the Hephthalite social world their religious traditions remain obscured by time. There is some evidence that Buddhism was practiced in some of the territories held by the Hephthalites; however, some contemporary authors also wrote of the persecution of Buddhists. There are references to sacred fire, which indicates at least some familiarity with Zoroastrianism. However, because Hephthalite graves have been found, not all aspects of Zoroastrianism would have been practiced, since the funerary ritual of this religion entailed leaving the body in the open to be devoured by sacred birds and dogs. As was the case with language religion was probably another area in which the Hephthalites constituted a multicultural society, with a number of faiths and belief systems being practiced at the same time."[438]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "As is the case with many aspects of the Hephthalite social world their religious traditions remain obscured by time. Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [0-1] ♥ "Summarizing, in the Hephthalite state was several religions: Buddhism, Zoroastrism, Christianity (basically Nestorianism), Manichaeism, Hinduism, as well as pagan views (the worship of the Sky, Fire). The Hephthalites held various religious views. It is difficult to say if one of religion dominated, because of the controversial nature of the sources. It seems that different religions co-existed and religious tolerance was characteristic in the Hephthalite empire, as it was later for the Turks and the Mongols in the time of Chingis khan." [446] Besides, no reference to human sacrifice was found in this source [447] which indicates that HS may be absent. That human sacrifice was not practiced by the Hephthalites may perhaps be inferred from the fact it was not commonly practiced in either in Hinduism, Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism. Buddhism categorically condemns the act of killing [448]. Greeks "talked and mythologized a lot about human sacrifice [...] but they don’t seem to have practiced it during the historical period.” [449] As for Zoroastrianism, there appears to be only one reference to human sacrifice among the Achaemenids (the best-known Zoroastrian polity among those preceding the Kushan empire), and it is an unreliable one [450]. And as for Hinduism, human sacrifice seems to be mostly associated with Vedic times and with nineteenth-century Goddess worship [451]. However, it may have been practiced in some of the other cults. Needs to be confirmed by an expert

Greeks "talked and mythologized a lot about human sacrifice [...] but they don’t seem to have practiced it during the historical period.” [485] As for Zoroastrianism, there appears to be only one reference to human sacrifice among the Achaemenids (the best-known Zoroastrian polity among those preceding the Kushan empire), and it is an unreliable one [486]. And as for Hinduism, human sacrifice seems to be mostly associated with Vedic times and with nineteenth-century Goddess worship [487]. However, it may have been practiced in some of the other cults. Needs to be confirmed by an expert

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ [monotheistic; polytheistic] ♥ "Summarizing, in the Hephthalite state was several religions: Buddhism, Zoroastrism, Christianity (basically Nestorianism), Manichaeism, Hinduism, as well as pagan views (the worship of the Sky, Fire). The Hephthalites held various religious views. It is difficult to say if one of religion dominated, because of the controversial nature of the sources. It seems that different religions co-existed and religious tolerance was characteristic in the Hephthalite empire, as it was later for the Turks and the Mongols in the time of Chingis khan." [509] In Hinduism: "According to one popular formula, there are 330 million gods in Hinduism. Nevertheless, most Hindus have believed that there is ultimately one deity, and that the diversity of other gods are his or her emanations or manifestations, representing his or her agency in particular contexts. [...] The three gods most commonly worshipped as the ultimate in Hindu traditions are Visnu, Siva, and the Goddess, variously called Devī, "Goddess," and Sakti, "Power." This triad of deities must be distinguished from an overlapping group of three deities who are conceived in the Puranas and other authoritative traditions to perform particular cosmic functions - Brahma, who creates the universe anew in each vast cosmic cycle, and also, in some accounts, bestows the Veda upon humanity; Visnu, who preserves the universe by intervening to rectify problems through his avataras, "descents" or "incarnations"; and Siva, who destroys the universe at the end of each cosmic cycle. [...] [W]hichever deity is conceived as the ultimate is understood to be the source who emanates the others, both the triad who perform the aforementioned cosmic functions and lower supernatural beings, and thus to be the ultimate agent in their actions. [...] There are numerous other important deities besides those frequently conceived as a monotheistic ultimate. One of the most important of these is Ganesa, a son of Siva with the head of an elephant, believed to be a protective figure that removes obstacles. Another is Hanuman, the monkey-god who helped Rama in his battle against Ravana, idealized for his devotion to Rama and Sīta. Other deities important in particular regions include the South Indian Ayappa, regarded as the son of Siva and a female avatara of Visnu; and smallpox goddesses such as the North Indian Sitala." [510] In Buddhism: “There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else.” [511] In Zoroastrianism: "Zarathustra had a particularly close relationship with Ahura Mazda, from whom he received revelatory visions (daēna-). His vision, expressed in the Gathas, included a radical transformation of traditional beliefs. In place of the pantheon he elevated Ahura Mazda to a position of supremacy that approaches monotheism and surrounded him with a group of abstract entities, the Amesa Spentas, all of whom perpetuate key concepts of Iranian religion as hypostases of Ahura Mazda."[512] In Manichaeism: "The approximately forty Manichean gods and goddesses mentioned in doctrinal and hymnic texts seem to bear witness to a markedly polytheistic theology. [...] But even the Manichean myth itself makes it clear that there is, strictly speaking, only one eternal and all-embracing god, limited only by the Evil, namely the Father of Greatness. All the other deities, whether they are specified as gods or merely called angels, are no more than his “evocations.” (Text T. II K. 2a, A. von Le Coq, ed., 1911 [1912], p. 22, calls the Father of Greatness the elder brother and sister of all the other gods.)"[513]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ "Summarizing, in the Hephthalite state was several religions: Buddhism, Zoroastrism, Christianity (basically Nestorianism), Manichaeism, Hinduism, as well as pagan views (the worship of the Sky, Fire). The Hephthalites held various religious views. It is difficult to say if one of religion dominated, because of the controversial nature of the sources. It seems that different religions co-existed and religious tolerance was characteristic in the Hephthalite empire, as it was later for the Turks and the Mongols in the time of Chingis khan." [514] In Hinduism: "Each Purana tends to focus on a specific deity as the Supreme Being responsible for creating and ordering the world. [...] Thus, [for example,] Krishna [...] is a form of the supreme God who creates, maintains, and destroys the universe. In the Bhagavad Gita , Arjuna is permitted to glimpse Krishna’s cosmic form. He sees the entire universe, the sun, stars, and planets, and all the people gathered on the battlefield within the body of Krishna. Krishna tells him that everything that exists is part of himself, but that he is not limited to everything that exists. In other words, just as the Vedic Purusha extended ten finger-lengths beyond the created universe, so too Krishna is the entire cosmos and more." [515] In Zoroastrianism: "The name of the supreme God of the Zoroastrian faith is Ahura Mazda (or Ohrmazd). He is self-created, omniscient, omnipresent, holy, invisible, and beyond human conceptualization (Yasna 4.7, 29.4, 31.13, 45.5; Yasht 1.7, 1.8, 1.12, 1.13-14; Videvdat 2.1, 19.20)."[516] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [517] In Christianity: “God exists without beginning or end, as the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent.” [518]

♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ "Summarizing, in the Hephthalite state was several religions: Buddhism, Zoroastrism, Christianity (basically Nestorianism), Manichaeism, Hinduism, as well as pagan views (the worship of the Sky, Fire).
